<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Exoplanet</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">  (Redirected from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Extrasolar_planet&amp;redirect=no" title="Extrasolar planet">Extrasolar planet</a>)</span></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the album by The Contortionist, see <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet_(album)" title="Exoplanet (album)">Exoplanet (album)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:304px;max-width:304px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Planets_everywhere_(artist%E2%80%99s_impression).jpg"><img alt="Size-exaggerated artist's conception showing the ratio of planets to stars in our galaxy" data-file-height="2392" data-file-width="3621" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Planets_everywhere_%28artist%E2%80%99s_impression%29.jpg/300px-Planets_everywhere_%28artist%E2%80%99s_impression%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Planets_everywhere_%28artist%E2%80%99s_impression%29.jpg/450px-Planets_everywhere_%28artist%E2%80%99s_impression%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Planets_everywhere_%28artist%E2%80%99s_impression%29.jpg/600px-Planets_everywhere_%28artist%E2%80%99s_impression%29.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Artist's impression of how commonly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planets" title="Planets">planets</a> orbit the <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Histogram_Chart_of_Discovered_Exoplanets_as_of_2017-03-08.png"><img alt="Histogram of Discovered Exoplanets each year as of March 8, 2017" data-file-height="1075" data-file-width="1781" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Histogram_Chart_of_Discovered_Exoplanets_as_of_2017-03-08.png/300px-Histogram_Chart_of_Discovered_Exoplanets_as_of_2017-03-08.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Histogram_Chart_of_Discovered_Exoplanets_as_of_2017-03-08.png/450px-Histogram_Chart_of_Discovered_Exoplanets_as_of_2017-03-08.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Histogram_Chart_of_Discovered_Exoplanets_as_of_2017-03-08.png/600px-Histogram_Chart_of_Discovered_Exoplanets_as_of_2017-03-08.png 2x" width="300"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Discovered exoplanets each year as of March 8, 2017<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="graph is based on own work without providing source material (June 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Exoplanet_Comparison_TrES-3_b.png"><img alt="Size comparison of Jupiter and the exoplanet TrES-3b" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="1040" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Exoplanet_Comparison_TrES-3_b.png/300px-Exoplanet_Comparison_TrES-3_b.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Exoplanet_Comparison_TrES-3_b.png/450px-Exoplanet_Comparison_TrES-3_b.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Exoplanet_Comparison_TrES-3_b.png/600px-Exoplanet_Comparison_TrES-3_b.png 2x" width="300"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Size comparison of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> and the exoplanet <a href="/wiki/TrES-3b" title="TrES-3b">TrES-3b</a>. TrES-3b has an orbital period of only 31 hours<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> and is classified as a <a href="/wiki/Hot_Jupiter" title="Hot Jupiter">Hot Jupiter</a> for being large and close to its star, making it one of the easiest planets to detect by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transit_method" title="Transit method">transit method</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>An <b>exoplanet</b> (<span class="nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">UK</a>:</small> <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/./: syllable break">.</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/./: syllable break">.</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <span class="nowrap"><small><a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">US</a>:</small> <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/./: syllable break">.</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/./: syllable break">.</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> or <b>extrasolar planet</b> is a planet outside of our <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">solar system</a> that orbits a star. The first evidence of an exoplanet was noted as early as 1917, but was not recognized as such.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20171101_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20171101-4">[4]</a></sup> However, the <a href="/wiki/Discoveries_of_exoplanets#1988.E2.80.931994" title="Discoveries of exoplanets">first scientific detection</a> of an exoplanet was in 1988. Shortly afterwards, the first confirmed detection was in 1992. As of 1 November 2017, there are 3,693 <a class="mw-selflink selflink">planets</a> in 2,768 <a href="/wiki/Planetary_system" title="Planetary system">systems</a>, with 620 <a href="/wiki/List_of_multiplanetary_systems" title="List of multiplanetary systems">systems</a> having more than one <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Exoplanet_Catalog_.28epc.29_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Exoplanet_Catalog_.28epc.29-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/High_Accuracy_Radial_Velocity_Planet_Searcher" title="High Accuracy Radial Velocity Planet Searcher">HARPS</a> (since 2004) has discovered about a hundred exoplanets while the <a href="/wiki/Kepler_(spacecraft)" title="Kepler (spacecraft)"><i>Kepler</i> space telescope</a> (since 2009) has found more than two thousand. <i>Kepler</i> has also detected a few thousand<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keplersite_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-keplersite-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usher_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-usher-7">[7]</a></sup> candidate planets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mygoditsfullofplanets_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-mygoditsfullofplanets-9">[9]</a></sup> of which about 11% may be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/False_positive" title="False positive">false positives</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> In several cases, <a href="/wiki/List_of_multiplanetary_systems" title="List of multiplanetary systems">multiple planets</a> have been observed around a star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature-20120111_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-20120111-11">[11]</a></sup> About 1 in 5 <a href="/wiki/Solar_analog" title="Solar analog">Sun-like stars</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteA_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteA-12">[a]</a></sup> have an "<a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>-sized"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteB_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteB-13">[b]</a></sup> planet in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitable_zone" title="Habitable zone">habitable zone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteC_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteC-14">[c]</a></sup> Assuming there are 200 billion stars in the Milky Way,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteD_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteD-15">[d]</a></sup> one can hypothesize that there are 11 billion potentially habitable Earth-sized planets in the Milky Way, rising to 40 billion if planets orbiting the numerous <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarfs</a> are included.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LATimes-20131104_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes-20131104-16">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/List_of_exoplanet_extremes#Planetary_characteristics" title="List of exoplanet extremes">least massive planet</a> known is <a href="/wiki/PSR_B1257%2B12_A" title="PSR B1257+12 A">Draugr</a> (also known as PSR B1257+12 A or PSR B1257+12 b), which is about twice the mass of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. The <a href="/wiki/List_of_exoplanet_extremes#Planetary_characteristics" title="List of exoplanet extremes">most massive planet</a> listed on the <a href="/wiki/NASA_Exoplanet_Archive" title="NASA Exoplanet Archive">NASA Exoplanet Archive</a> is <a href="/wiki/DENIS-P_J082303.1-491201_b" title="DENIS-P J082303.1-491201 b">DENIS-P J082303.1-491201 b</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CT-Exo-2014_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-CT-Exo-2014-17">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HU-201308_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-HU-201308-18">[14]</a></sup> about 29 times the mass of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, although according to some definitions of a <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a>, it is too massive to be a planet and may be a <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">brown dwarf</a> instead. There are planets that are so near to their star that they take only a few hours to orbit and there are others so far away that they take thousands of years to orbit. Some are so far out that it is difficult to tell whether they are gravitationally bound to the star. Almost all of the planets detected so far are within the Milky Way, but there have also been a few possible detections of <a href="/wiki/Extragalactic_planet" title="Extragalactic planet">extragalactic planets</a>. The nearest exoplanet is <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri_b" title="Proxima Centauri b">Proxima Centauri b</a>, located 4.2 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> (1.3 <a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">parsecs</a>) from Earth and orbiting <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">Proxima Centauri</a>, the closest star to the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>The discovery of exoplanets has intensified interest in the search for <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial life</a>. There is special interest in planets that orbit in a star's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitable_zone" title="Habitable zone">habitable zone</a>, where it is possible for liquid water, a prerequisite for <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> on Earth, to exist on the surface. The study of <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">planetary habitability</a> also considers a wide range of other factors in determining the suitability of a planet for hosting life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150106-DB_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150106-DB-20">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Besides exoplanets, there are also <a href="/wiki/Rogue_planet" title="Rogue planet">rogue planets</a>, which do not orbit any star and which tend to be considered separately, especially if they are <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giants</a>, in which case they are often counted, like <a href="/wiki/WISE_0855%E2%88%920714" title="WISE 0855−0714">WISE 0855−0714</a>, as <a href="/wiki/Sub-brown_dwarf" title="Sub-brown dwarf">sub-brown dwarfs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[17]</a></sup> The rogue planets in the Milky Way possibly number in the billions (or more).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exoplanet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_unusual_exoplanet_HIP_65426b_%E2%80%94_SPHERE%27s_firs.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="1280" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/The_unusual_exoplanet_HIP_65426b_%E2%80%94_SPHERE%27s_firs.jpg/220px-The_unusual_exoplanet_HIP_65426b_%E2%80%94_SPHERE%27s_firs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/The_unusual_exoplanet_HIP_65426b_%E2%80%94_SPHERE%27s_firs.jpg/330px-The_unusual_exoplanet_HIP_65426b_%E2%80%94_SPHERE%27s_firs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/The_unusual_exoplanet_HIP_65426b_%E2%80%94_SPHERE%27s_firs.jpg/440px-The_unusual_exoplanet_HIP_65426b_%E2%80%94_SPHERE%27s_firs.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_unusual_exoplanet_HIP_65426b_%E2%80%94_SPHERE%27s_firs.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Exoplanet <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=HIP_65426b&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="HIP 65426b (page does not exist)">HIP 65426b</a> is the first discovered planet around star <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=HIP_65426&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="HIP 65426 (page does not exist)">HIP 65426</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[20]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet_naming_convention" title="Exoplanet naming convention">Exoplanet naming convention</a></div>
<p>The convention for designating exoplanets is an extension of the system used for designating multiple-star systems as adopted by the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> (IAU). For exoplanets orbiting a single star, the designation is normally formed by taking the name or, more commonly, designation of its parent star and adding a lower case letter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[21]</a></sup> The first planet discovered in a system is given the designation "b" (the parent star is considered to be "a") and later planets are given subsequent letters. If several planets in the same system are discovered at the same time, the closest one to the star gets the next letter, followed by the other planets in order of orbital size. A provisional IAU-sanctioned standard exists to accommodate the designation of <a href="/wiki/Circumbinary_planet" title="Circumbinary planet">circumbinary planets</a>. A limited number of exoplanets have IAU-sanctioned proper names. Other naming systems exist.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_detection">History of detection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exoplanet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History of detection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>For centuries scientists, philosophers, and science fiction writers suspected that extrasolar planets existed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[22]</a></sup> but there was no way of detecting them or of knowing their frequency or how similar they might be to the planets of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. Various detection claims made in the nineteenth century were rejected by astronomers. The first evidence of an exoplanet was noted as early as 1917, but was not recognized as such.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20171101_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20171101-4">[4]</a></sup> However, the <a href="/wiki/Discoveries_of_exoplanets#1988.E2.80.931994" title="Discoveries of exoplanets">first scientific detection</a> of an exoplanet began in 1988. Shortly afterwards, the first confirmed detection came in 1992, with the discovery of several terrestrial-mass planets orbiting the <a href="/wiki/Pulsar" title="Pulsar">pulsar</a> <a href="/wiki/PSR_B1257%2B12" title="PSR B1257+12">PSR B1257+12</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolszczan_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wolszczan-27">[23]</a></sup> The first confirmation of an exoplanet orbiting a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Main-sequence" title="Main-sequence">main-sequence</a> star was made in 1995, when a giant planet was found in a four-day orbit around the nearby star <a href="/wiki/51_Pegasi" title="51 Pegasi">51 Pegasi</a>. Some exoplanets have been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direct_imaging" title="Direct imaging">imaged directly</a> by telescopes, but the vast majority have been detected through indirect methods, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transit_method" title="Transit method">transit method</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Doppler_spectroscopy" title="Doppler spectroscopy">radial-velocity method</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_speculations">Early speculations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exoplanet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early speculations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="rquote floatright" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:right ; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:33%;">
<tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">“</td>
<td style="padding:0 10px;">This space we declare to be infinite... In it are an infinity of worlds of the same kind as our own.</td>
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:1em; text-align:right;"><cite>— Giordano Bruno (1584)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[24]</a></sup></cite></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In the sixteenth century the Italian philosopher <a href="/wiki/Giordano_Bruno" title="Giordano Bruno">Giordano Bruno</a>, an early supporter of the <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Copernican</a> theory that Earth and other planets orbit the Sun (<a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">heliocentrism</a>), put forward the view that the fixed stars are similar to the Sun and are likewise accompanied by planets.</p>
<p>In the eighteenth century the same possibility was mentioned by <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> in the "<a href="/wiki/General_Scholium" title="General Scholium">General Scholium</a>" that concludes his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophiae_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophiae Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Principia</a></i>. Making a comparison to the Sun's planets, he wrote "And if the fixed stars are the centres of similar systems, they will all be constructed according to a similar design and subject to the dominion of <i>One</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1952, more than 40 years before the first <a href="/wiki/Hot_Jupiter" title="Hot Jupiter">hot Jupiter</a> was discovered, <a href="/wiki/Otto_Struve" title="Otto Struve">Otto Struve</a> wrote that there is no compelling reason why planets could not be much closer to their parent star than is the case in the Solar System, and proposed that <a href="/wiki/Doppler_spectroscopy" title="Doppler spectroscopy">Doppler spectroscopy</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transit_method" title="Transit method">transit method</a> could detect <a href="/wiki/Super-Jupiter" title="Super-Jupiter">super-Jupiters</a> in short orbits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discredited_claims">Discredited claims</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exoplanet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Discredited claims">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Claims of exoplanet detections have been made since the nineteenth century. Some of the earliest involve the <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">binary star</a> <a href="/wiki/70_Ophiuchi" title="70 Ophiuchi">70 Ophiuchi</a>. In 1855 <a href="/wiki/William_Stephen_Jacob" title="William Stephen Jacob">William Stephen Jacob</a> at the <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Madras_Observatory" title="Madras Observatory">Madras Observatory</a> reported that orbital anomalies made it "highly probable" that there was a "planetary body" in this system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[27]</a></sup> In the 1890s, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_Jackson_See" title="Thomas Jefferson Jackson See">Thomas J. J. See</a> of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Observatory" title="United States Naval Observatory">United States Naval Observatory</a> stated that the orbital anomalies proved the existence of a dark body in the 70 Ophiuchi system with a 36-year <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">period</a> around one of the stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[28]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Forest_Ray_Moulton" title="Forest Ray Moulton">Forest Ray Moulton</a> published a paper proving that a three-body system with those orbital parameters would be highly unstable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[29]</a></sup> During the 1950s and 1960s, <a href="/wiki/Peter_van_de_Kamp" title="Peter van de Kamp">Peter van de Kamp</a> of <a href="/wiki/Swarthmore_College" title="Swarthmore College">Swarthmore College</a> made another prominent series of detection claims, this time for planets orbiting <a href="/wiki/Barnard%27s_Star" title="Barnard's Star">Barnard's Star</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[30]</a></sup> Astronomers now generally regard all the early reports of detection as erroneous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boss_book_p31_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-boss_book_p31-35">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1991 <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Lyne" title="Andrew Lyne">Andrew Lyne</a>, M. Bailes and S. L. Shemar claimed to have discovered a <a href="/wiki/Pulsar_planet" title="Pulsar planet">pulsar planet</a> in orbit around <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PSR_1829-10" title="PSR 1829-10">PSR 1829-10</a>, using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulsar_timing" title="Pulsar timing">pulsar timing</a> variations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LyneBailes_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-LyneBailes-36">[32]</a></sup> The claim briefly received intense attention, but Lyne and his team soon retracted it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LyneRetraction_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-LyneRetraction-37">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Confirmed_discoveries">Confirmed discoveries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exoplanet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Confirmed discoveries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Discoveries_of_exoplanets" title="Discoveries of exoplanets">Discoveries of exoplanets</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_exoplanet_firsts" title="List of exoplanet firsts">List of exoplanet firsts</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:444226main_exoplanet20100414-a-full.jpg"><img alt="False-color, star-subtracted, direct image using a vortex coronagraph of 3 exoplanets around star HR8799" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/444226main_exoplanet20100414-a-full.jpg/200px-444226main_exoplanet20100414-a-full.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/444226main_exoplanet20100414-a-full.jpg/300px-444226main_exoplanet20100414-a-full.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/444226main_exoplanet20100414-a-full.jpg/400px-444226main_exoplanet20100414-a-full.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The three known planets of the star <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HR8799" title="HR8799">HR8799</a>, as imaged by the <a href="/wiki/Hale_Telescope" title="Hale Telescope">Hale Telescope</a>. The light from the central star was blanked out by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_vortex_coronagraph" title="Vector vortex coronagraph">vector vortex coronagraph</a>.</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brown_dwarf_2M_J044144_and_planet.jpg"><img alt="Hubble image of brown dwarf 2MASS J044144 and its 5–10 Jupiter-mass companion, before and after star-subtraction" data-file-height="628" data-file-width="1000" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Brown_dwarf_2M_J044144_and_planet.jpg/200px-Brown_dwarf_2M_J044144_and_planet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Brown_dwarf_2M_J044144_and_planet.jpg/300px-Brown_dwarf_2M_J044144_and_planet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Brown_dwarf_2M_J044144_and_planet.jpg/400px-Brown_dwarf_2M_J044144_and_planet.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2MASS_J044144" title="2MASS J044144">2MASS J044144</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">brown dwarf</a> with a companion about 5–10 times the mass of Jupiter. It is not clear whether this companion object is a <a href="/wiki/Sub-brown_dwarf" title="Sub-brown dwarf">sub-brown dwarf</a> or a planet.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As of 1 November 2017, a total of 3,693 confirmed exoplanets are listed in the Extrasolar Planets Encyclopaedia, including a few that were confirmations of controversial claims from the late 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Exoplanet_Catalog_.28epc.29_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Exoplanet_Catalog_.28epc.29-5">[5]</a></sup> The first published discovery to receive subsequent confirmation was made in 1988 by the Canadian astronomers Bruce Campbell, G. A. H. Walker, and Stephenson Yang of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Victoria" title="University of Victoria">University of Victoria</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_British_Columbia" title="University of British Columbia">University of British Columbia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell-38">[34]</a></sup> Although they were cautious about claiming a planetary detection, their radial-velocity observations suggested that a planet orbits the star <a href="/wiki/Gamma_Cephei" title="Gamma Cephei">Gamma Cephei</a>. Partly because the observations were at the very limits of instrumental capabilities at the time, astronomers remained skeptical for several years about this and other similar observations. It was thought some of the apparent planets might instead have been <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">brown dwarfs</a>, objects intermediate in mass between planets and stars. In 1990 additional observations were published that supported the existence of the planet orbiting Gamma Cephei,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[35]</a></sup> but subsequent work in 1992 again raised serious doubts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walker-40">[36]</a></sup> Finally, in 2003, improved techniques allowed the planet's existence to be confirmed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[37]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Star_AB_Pictoris_and_its_Companion_-_Phot-14d-05-normal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="798" data-file-width="800" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/The_Star_AB_Pictoris_and_its_Companion_-_Phot-14d-05-normal.jpg/150px-The_Star_AB_Pictoris_and_its_Companion_-_Phot-14d-05-normal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/The_Star_AB_Pictoris_and_its_Companion_-_Phot-14d-05-normal.jpg/225px-The_Star_AB_Pictoris_and_its_Companion_-_Phot-14d-05-normal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/The_Star_AB_Pictoris_and_its_Companion_-_Phot-14d-05-normal.jpg/300px-The_Star_AB_Pictoris_and_its_Companion_-_Phot-14d-05-normal.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Star_AB_Pictoris_and_its_Companion_-_Phot-14d-05-normal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Coronagraph" title="Coronagraph">Coronagraphic</a> image of <a href="/wiki/AB_Pictoris" title="AB Pictoris">AB Pictoris</a> showing a companion (bottom left), which is either a brown dwarf or a massive planet. The data was obtained on 16 March 2003 with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_instruments_at_the_Very_Large_Telescope" title="List of instruments at the Very Large Telescope">NACO</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Very_Large_Telescope" title="Very Large Telescope">VLT</a>, using a 1.4 arcsec occulting mask on top of AB Pictoris.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 9 January 1992, radio astronomers <a href="/wiki/Aleksander_Wolszczan" title="Aleksander Wolszczan">Aleksander Wolszczan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dale_Frail" title="Dale Frail">Dale Frail</a> announced the discovery of two planets orbiting the <a href="/wiki/Pulsar" title="Pulsar">pulsar</a> <a href="/wiki/PSR_B1257%2B12" title="PSR B1257+12">PSR 1257+12</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolszczan_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wolszczan-27">[23]</a></sup> This discovery was confirmed, and is generally considered to be the first definitive detection of exoplanets. Follow-up observations solidified these results, and confirmation of a third planet in 1994 revived the topic in the popular press.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[38]</a></sup> These pulsar planets are thought to have formed from the unusual remnants of the <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a> that produced the pulsar, in a second round of planet formation, or else to be the <a href="/wiki/Chthonian_planet" title="Chthonian planet">remaining rocky cores</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giants</a> that somehow survived the supernova and then decayed into their current orbits.</p>
<p>On 6 October 1995, <a href="/wiki/Michel_Mayor" title="Michel Mayor">Michel Mayor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Didier_Queloz" title="Didier Queloz">Didier Queloz</a> of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Geneva" title="University of Geneva">University of Geneva</a> announced the first definitive detection of <a href="/wiki/51_Pegasi_b" title="51 Pegasi b">an exoplanet</a> orbiting a <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main-sequence</a> star, namely the nearby <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G-type_star" title="G-type star">G-type star</a> <a href="/wiki/51_Pegasi" title="51 Pegasi">51 Pegasi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayor_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mayor-43">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gibn_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-gibn-44">[40]</a></sup> This discovery, made at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Observatoire_de_Haute-Provence" title="Observatoire de Haute-Provence">Observatoire de Haute-Provence</a>, ushered in the modern era of exoplanetary discovery. Technological advances, most notably in high-resolution <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">spectroscopy</a>, led to the rapid detection of many new exoplanets: astronomers could detect exoplanets indirectly by measuring their <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravitational</a> influence on the motion of their host stars. More extrasolar planets were later detected by observing the variation in a star's apparent luminosity as an orbiting planet passed in front of it.</p>
<p>Initially, most known exoplanets were massive planets that orbited very close to their parent stars. Astronomers were surprised by these "<a href="/wiki/Hot_Jupiter" title="Hot Jupiter">hot Jupiters</a>", because theories of <a href="/wiki/Nebular_hypothesis#Formation_of_planets" title="Nebular hypothesis">planetary formation</a> had indicated that giant planets should only form at large distances from stars. But eventually more planets of other sorts were found, and it is now clear that hot Jupiters make up the minority of exoplanets. In 1999, <a href="/wiki/Upsilon_Andromedae" title="Upsilon Andromedae">Upsilon Andromedae</a> became the first main-sequence star known to have multiple planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lissauer_multiple99_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lissauer_multiple99-45">[41]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kepler-16" title="Kepler-16">Kepler-16</a> contains the first discovered planet that orbits around a binary main-sequence star system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doyle_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Doyle-46">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 26 February 2014, NASA announced the discovery of 715 newly verified exoplanets around 305 stars by the <a href="/wiki/Kepler_(spacecraft)" title="Kepler (spacecraft)">Kepler Space Telescope</a>. These exoplanets were checked using a statistical technique called "verification by multiplicity".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kepler1700_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-kepler1700-47">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-space.com_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-space.com-48">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcnews_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbcnews-49">[45]</a></sup> Prior to these results, most confirmed planets were gas giants comparable in size to Jupiter or larger as they are more easily detected, but the Kepler planets are mostly between the size of Neptune and the size of Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kepler1700_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-kepler1700-47">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 23 July 2015, NASA announced <a href="/wiki/Kepler-452b" title="Kepler-452b">Kepler-452b</a>, a near-Earth-size planet orbiting the habitable zone of a G2-type star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20150723_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20150723-50">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Candidate_discoveries">Candidate discoveries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exoplanet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Candidate discoveries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As of June 2017, NASA's <a href="/wiki/Kepler_(spacecraft)" title="Kepler (spacecraft)">Kepler mission</a> had identified more than 5,000 <a href="/wiki/Kepler_(spacecraft)#Extrasolar_planets_detected" title="Kepler (spacecraft)">planetary candidates</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20170612_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20170612-51">[47]</a></sup> several of them being nearly Earth-sized and located in the habitable zone, some around Sun-like stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keplersite_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-keplersite-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usher_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-usher-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-ExoplanetArch_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-ExoplanetArch-52">[48]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tnone center">

</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Methodology">Methodology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exoplanet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Methodology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The first exoplanet was detected on 6 October 1995, and was named <a href="/wiki/51_Pegasi_b" title="51 Pegasi b">51 Pegasi b</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[51]</a></sup> When an extrasolar planet is observed to <a href="/wiki/Transit_(astronomy)" title="Transit (astronomy)">transit</a> its parent star, astronomers are able to assess some physical properties of the planet from an interstellar distance, including <a href="/wiki/Planetary_mass" title="Planetary mass">planetary mass</a> and size, which in turn provide fundamental constraints on models of their physical structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Charbonneau_2008_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Charbonneau_2008-56">[52]</a></sup> Furthermore, such events afford the opportunity to study the dynamics and chemistry of its <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet#Atmosphere" title="Exoplanet">atmosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Charbonneau_2008_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Charbonneau_2008-56">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>Statistical surveys and individual characterization are the keys to addressing the fundamental questions in exoplanetology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Desert2012_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Desert2012-57">[53]</a></sup> As of August 2016, varying techniques have been used to discover 3,502 exoplanets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[54]</a></sup> Documenting the properties of a large sample exoplanets at various ages, orbiting their parent stars of <a href="/wiki/Stellar_classification" title="Stellar classification">various types</a>, will contribute to increased understanding —or better models— of planetary formation (<a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accretion</a>), geological evolution, orbit migration,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Desert2012_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Desert2012-57">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kraus_2012_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kraus_2012-59">[55]</a></sup> and their potential habitability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ollivier2014_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ollivier2014-60">[56]</a></sup> Characterizing the atmospheres of extrasolar planets is the new frontier in exoplanetary science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Detection_techniques">Detection techniques</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exoplanet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Detection techniques">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Methods_of_detecting_exoplanets" title="Methods of detecting exoplanets">Methods of detecting exoplanets</a></div>
<p>About 97% of all the confirmed exoplanets have been discovered by indirect techniques of detection, mainly by radial velocity measurements and transit monitoring techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ollivier2014_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ollivier2014-60">[56]</a></sup> The following methods have proved successful for discovering a new planet or confirming an already discovered planet:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[58]</a></sup></p>
<div class="div-col columns column-count column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2;">
<ul>
<li>Radial velocity</li>
<li>Gravitational microlensing</li>
<li>Direct imaging</li>
<li>Polarimetry</li>
<li>Astrometry</li>
<li>Transit photometry
<ul>
<li>Reflection/emission modulations</li>
<li>Light variations due to relativistic beaming</li>
<li>Light variations due to ellipsoidal variations</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Timing variations
<ul>
<li>Pulsar timing</li>
<li>Variable star timing</li>
<li>Transit timing variation method</li>
<li>Transit duration variation method</li>
<li>Eclipsing binary minima timing</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
</div>
<p><br/></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_and_evolution">Formation and evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exoplanet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Formation and evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">Accretion (astrophysics)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nebular_hypothesis" title="Nebular hypothesis">Nebular hypothesis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Planetary_migration" title="Planetary migration">Planetary migration</a></div>
<p>Planets form within a few tens of millions of years of their star forming,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[61]</a></sup> and there are stars that are forming today and other stars that are ten billion years old. Studying exoplanets allows the observation of planets at different stages of their evolution, whereas the planets of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> can be observed only as they are today. When planets form, they have <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> envelopes that cool and contract over time and, depending on the mass of the planet, some or all of the hydrogen is eventually lost to space. This means that even terrestrial planets can start off with large radii.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[64]</a></sup> An example is Kepler-51b which has only about twice the mass of Earth but is almost the size of Saturn which is a hundred times the mass of Earth. Kepler-51b is quite young at a few hundred million years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kepler-51_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-kepler-51-69">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Planet-hosting_stars">Planet-hosting stars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exoplanet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Planet-hosting stars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Planetary_system#Planet-hosting_stars" title="Planetary system">Planetary system § Planet-hosting stars</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Morgan-Keenan_spectral_classification.png"><img alt="The Morgan-Keenan spectral classification system, showing size-and-color comparisons of M, K, G, F, A, B, and O stars" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="2117" height="80" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Morgan-Keenan_spectral_classification.png/220px-Morgan-Keenan_spectral_classification.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Morgan-Keenan_spectral_classification.png/330px-Morgan-Keenan_spectral_classification.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Morgan-Keenan_spectral_classification.png/440px-Morgan-Keenan_spectral_classification.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Morgan-Keenan_spectral_classification.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Morgan-Keenan spectral classification</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_exoplanet_orbiting_two_stars.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_exoplanet_orbiting_two_stars.jpg/220px-Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_exoplanet_orbiting_two_stars.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_exoplanet_orbiting_two_stars.jpg/330px-Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_exoplanet_orbiting_two_stars.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_exoplanet_orbiting_two_stars.jpg/440px-Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_exoplanet_orbiting_two_stars.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artist%E2%80%99s_impression_of_exoplanet_orbiting_two_stars.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist’s impression of exoplanet orbiting two stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[66]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There is at least one planet on average per star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nature-20120111_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-20120111-11">[11]</a></sup> About 1 in 5 <a href="/wiki/Solar_analog" title="Solar analog">Sun-like stars</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteA_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteA-12">[a]</a></sup> have an "Earth-sized"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteB_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteB-13">[b]</a></sup> planet in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitable_zone" title="Habitable zone">habitable zone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-earthsunhzprev_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-earthsunhzprev-71">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most known exoplanets orbit stars roughly similar to the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main-sequence stars</a> of <a href="/wiki/Stellar_classification" title="Stellar classification">spectral categories</a> F, G, or K. Lower-mass stars (<a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarfs</a>, of <a href="/wiki/Stellar_classification" title="Stellar classification">spectral category</a> M) are less likely to have planets massive enough to be detected by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radial-velocity_method" title="Radial-velocity method">radial-velocity method</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cumming08_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-cumming08-72">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bonfils05_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-bonfils05-73">[69]</a></sup> Despite this, several tens of planets around red dwarfs have been discovered by the <a href="/wiki/Kepler_(spacecraft)" title="Kepler (spacecraft)"><i>Kepler</i> spacecraft</a>, which uses the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transit_method" title="Transit method">transit method</a> to detect smaller planets.</p>
<p>Stars with a higher <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metallicity</a> than the Sun are more likely to have planets, especially giant planets, than stars with lower metallicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some planets orbit one member of a <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">binary star</a> system,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[71]</a></sup> and several <a href="/wiki/Circumbinary_planet" title="Circumbinary planet">circumbinary planets</a> have been discovered which orbit around both members of binary star. A few planets in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triple_star" title="Triple star">triple star</a> systems are known<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[72]</a></sup> and one in the quadruple system <a href="/wiki/PH1b" title="PH1b">Kepler-64</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="General_features">General features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exoplanet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: General features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Color_and_brightness">Color and brightness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exoplanet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Color and brightness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sudarsky%27s_gas_giant_classification" title="Sudarsky's gas giant classification">Sudarsky's gas giant classification</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Color_HD_189733b_vs_solar_system.jpg"><img alt="Color-color diagram comparing the colors of Solar System planets to exoplanet HD 189733b. HD 189733b reflects as much green as Mars and almost as much blue as Earth." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="474" data-file-width="456" height="416" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Color_HD_189733b_vs_solar_system.jpg/400px-Color_HD_189733b_vs_solar_system.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b1/Color_HD_189733b_vs_solar_system.jpg 1.5x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Color_HD_189733b_vs_solar_system.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This <a href="/wiki/Color%E2%80%93color_diagram" title="Color–color diagram">color–color diagram</a> compares the colors of planets in the Solar System to exoplanet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HD_189733b" title="HD 189733b">HD 189733b</a>. The exoplanet's deep blue color is produced by <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicate</a> droplets, which scatter blue light in its atmosphere.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2013 the color of an exoplanet was determined for the first time. The best-fit <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> measurements of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HD_189733b" title="HD 189733b">HD 189733b</a> suggest that it is deep dark blue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Visually, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GJ_504_b" title="GJ 504 b">GJ 504 b</a> would have a magenta color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuzuhara2013_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuzuhara2013-79">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Kappa_Andromedae_b" title="Kappa Andromedae b">Kappa Andromedae b</a>, if seen up close, would appear reddish in color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arXiv1211.3744_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-arXiv1211.3744-80">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>The apparent brightness (<a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a>) of a planet depends on how far away the observer is, how reflective the planet is (<a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a>), and how much light the planet receives from its star, which depends on how far the planet is from the star and how bright the star is. So, a planet with a low albedo that is close to its star can appear brighter than a planet with high albedo that is far from the star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>The darkest known planet in terms of <a href="/wiki/Geometric_albedo" title="Geometric albedo">geometric albedo</a> is <a href="/wiki/TrES-2b" title="TrES-2b">TrES-2b</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Hot_Jupiter" title="Hot Jupiter">hot Jupiter</a> that reflects less than 1% of the light from its star, making it less reflective than coal or black acrylic paint. Hot Jupiters are expected to be quite dark due to sodium and potassium in their atmospheres but it is not known why TrES-2b is so dark—it could be due to an unknown chemical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-darkest_news_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-darkest_news-82">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>For <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giants</a>, geometric albedo generally decreases with increasing metallicity or atmospheric temperature unless there are clouds to modify this effect. Increased cloud-column depth increases the albedo at optical wavelengths, but decreases it at some infrared wavelengths. Optical albedo increases with age, because older planets have higher cloud-column depths. Optical albedo decreases with increasing mass, because higher-mass giant planets have higher surface gravities, which produces lower cloud-column depths. Also, elliptical orbits can cause major fluctuations in atmospheric composition, which can have a significant effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wfirstreturn_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-wfirstreturn-85">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is more thermal emission than reflection at some near-infrared wavelengths for massive and/or young gas giants. So, although optical brightness is fully <a href="/wiki/Planetary_phase" title="Planetary phase">phase</a>-dependent, this is not always the case in the near infrared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wfirstreturn_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-wfirstreturn-85">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>Temperatures of gas giants reduce over time and with distance from their star. Lowering the temperature increases optical albedo even without clouds. At a sufficiently low temperature, water clouds form, which further increase optical albedo. At even lower temperatures ammonia clouds form, resulting in the highest albedos at most optical and near-infrared wavelengths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wfirstreturn_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-wfirstreturn-85">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_field">Magnetic field</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exoplanet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Magnetic field">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2014, a magnetic field around <a href="/wiki/HD_209458_b" title="HD 209458 b">HD 209458 b</a> was inferred from the way hydrogen was evaporating from the planet. It is the first (indirect) detection of a magnetic field on an exoplanet. The magnetic field is estimated to be about one tenth as strong as Jupiter's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>Interaction between a close-in planet's magnetic field and a star can produce spots on the star in a similar way to how the <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a> produce aurorae on Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[84]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">Auroral</a> <a href="/wiki/Radio" title="Radio">radio</a> emissions could be detected with radio telescopes such as <a href="/wiki/LOFAR" title="LOFAR">LOFAR</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[86]</a></sup> The radio emissions could enable determination of the rotation rate of a planet which is difficult to detect otherwise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Earth's magnetic field results from its flowing liquid metallic core, but in massive super-Earths with high pressure, different compounds may form which do not match those created under terrestrial conditions. Compounds may form with greater viscosities and high melting temperatures which could prevent the interiors from separating into different layers and so result in undifferentiated coreless mantles. Forms of magnesium oxide such as MgSi<sub>3</sub>O<sub>12</sub> could be a liquid metal at the pressures and temperatures found in super-Earths and could generate a magnetic field in the mantles of super-Earths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kean_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kean-92">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hot_Jupiter" title="Hot Jupiter">Hot Jupiters</a> have been observed to have a larger radius than expected. This could be caused by the interaction between the <a href="/wiki/Stellar_wind" title="Stellar wind">stellar wind</a> and the planet's magnetosphere creating an <a href="/wiki/Joule_heating" title="Joule heating">electric current through the planet that heats</a> it up causing it to expand. The more magnetically active a star is the greater the stellar wind and the larger the electric current leading to more heating and expansion of the planet. This theory matches the observation that stellar activity is correlated with inflated planetary radii.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plate_tectonics">Plate tectonics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exoplanet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Plate tectonics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2007 two independent teams of researchers came to opposing conclusions about the likelihood of <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a> on larger <a href="/wiki/Super-Earth" title="Super-Earth">super-Earths</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[92]</a></sup> with one team saying that plate tectonics would be episodic or stagnant<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[93]</a></sup> and the other team saying that plate tectonics is very likely on super-Earths even if the planet is dry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>If super-Earths have more than 80 times as much water as Earth then they become <a href="/wiki/Ocean_planet" title="Ocean planet">ocean planets</a> with all land completely submerged. However, if there is less water than this limit, then the deep water cycle will move enough water between the oceans and mantle to allow continents to exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Volcanism">Volcanism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exoplanet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Volcanism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Large surface temperature variations on <a href="/wiki/55_Cancri_e" title="55 Cancri e">55 Cancri e</a> have been attributed to possible volcanic activity releasing large clouds of dust which blanket the planet and block thermal emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rings">Rings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exoplanet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Rings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The star <a href="/wiki/1SWASP_J140747.93-394542.6" title="1SWASP J140747.93-394542.6">1SWASP J140747.93-394542.6</a> is orbited by an object that is circled by a <a href="/wiki/Ring_system" title="Ring system">ring system</a> much larger than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturn%27s_rings" title="Saturn's rings">Saturn's rings</a>. However, the mass of the object is not known; it could be a brown dwarf or low-mass star instead of a planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>The brightness of optical images of <a href="/wiki/Fomalhaut_b" title="Fomalhaut b">Fomalhaut b</a> could be due to starlight reflecting off a circumplanetary ring system with a radius between 20 and 40 times that of Jupiter's radius, about the size of the orbits of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galilean_moon" title="Galilean moon">Galilean moons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalas2008_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kalas2008-105">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>The rings of the Solar System's gas giants are aligned with their planet's equator. However, for exoplanets that orbit close to their star, tidal forces from the star would lead to the outermost rings of a planet being aligned with the planet's orbital plane around the star. A planet's innermost rings would still be aligned with the planet's equator so that if the planet has a <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">tilted rotational axis</a>, then the different alignments between the inner and outer rings would create a warped ring system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moons">Moons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exoplanet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Moons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In December 2013 a candidate <a href="/wiki/Exomoon" title="Exomoon">exomoon</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Rogue_planet" title="Rogue planet">rogue planet</a> was announced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[103]</a></sup> No exomoons have been confirmed so far.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheres">Atmospheres</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exoplanet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Atmospheres">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exoplanet_atmosphere" title="Exoplanet atmosphere">Exoplanet atmosphere</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cloudy_versus_clear_atmospheres_on_two_exoplanets.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3400" data-file-width="4200" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Cloudy_versus_clear_atmospheres_on_two_exoplanets.jpg/220px-Cloudy_versus_clear_atmospheres_on_two_exoplanets.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Cloudy_versus_clear_atmospheres_on_two_exoplanets.jpg/330px-Cloudy_versus_clear_atmospheres_on_two_exoplanets.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Cloudy_versus_clear_atmospheres_on_two_exoplanets.jpg/440px-Cloudy_versus_clear_atmospheres_on_two_exoplanets.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cloudy_versus_clear_atmospheres_on_two_exoplanets.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Clear versus cloudy atmospheres on two exoplanets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[104]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Atmospheres have been detected around several exoplanets. The first to be observed was <a href="/wiki/HD_209458_b" title="HD 209458 b">HD 209458 b</a> in 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KIC_12557548_b" title="KIC 12557548 b">KIC 12557548 b</a> is a small rocky planet, very close to its star, that is evaporating and leaving a trailing tail of cloud and dust like a <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[106]</a></sup> The dust could be ash erupting from volcanos and escaping due to the small planet's low surface-gravity, or it could be from metals that are vaporized by the high temperatures of being so close to the star with the metal vapor then condensing into dust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>In June 2015, scientists reported that the atmosphere of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GJ_436_b" title="GJ 436 b">GJ 436 b</a> was evaporating, resulting in a giant cloud around the planet and, due to radiation from the host star, a long trailing tail 14<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>6</sup> km (9<span style="margin-left:0.2em">×<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>6</sup> mi) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150625_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150625-112">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May 2017, glints of light from <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, seen as twinkling from an orbiting satellite a million miles away, were found to be <a href="/wiki/Reflection_(physics)" title="Reflection (physics)">reflected light</a> from <a href="/wiki/Ice_crystals" title="Ice crystals">ice crystals</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">atmosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20170519_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20170519-113">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GRL-201760515_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-GRL-201760515-114">[110]</a></sup> The technology used to determine this may be useful in studying the atmospheres of distant worlds, including those of exoplanets.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Insolation_pattern">Insolation pattern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exoplanet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Insolation pattern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tidally_locked" title="Tidally locked">Tidally locked</a> planets in a 1:1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin%E2%80%93orbit_resonance" title="Spin–orbit resonance">spin–orbit resonance</a> would have their star always shining directly overhead on one spot which would be hot with the opposite hemisphere receiving no light and being freezing cold. Such a planet could resemble an eyeball with the hotspot being the pupil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[111]</a></sup> Planets with an <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentric orbit</a> could be locked in other resonances. 3:2 and 5:2 resonances would result in a double-eyeball pattern with hotspots in both eastern and western hemispheres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[112]</a></sup> Planets with both an eccentric orbit and a <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">tilted axis of rotation</a> would have more complicated insolation patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[113]</a></sup></p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">Astrobiology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Circumstellar_habitable_zone" title="Circumstellar habitable zone">Circumstellar habitable zone</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">Planetary habitability</a></div>
<p>As more planets are discovered, the field of exoplanetology continues to grow into a deeper study of extrasolar worlds, and will ultimately tackle the prospect of <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">life on planets</a> beyond the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ollivier2014_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ollivier2014-60">[56]</a></sup> At cosmic distances, <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> can only be detected if it is developed at a planetary scale and strongly modified the planetary environment, in such a way that the modifications cannot be explained by classical physico-chemical processes (out of equilibrium processes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ollivier2014_60-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ollivier2014-60">[56]</a></sup> For example, molecular <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br/>
2</span></span>) in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">atmosphere of Earth</a> is a result of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> by living plants and many kinds of microorganisms, so it can be used as an <a href="/wiki/Biomarker" title="Biomarker">indication of life</a> on exoplanets, although small amounts of oxygen could also be produced by non-biological means.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAOJ2014_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAOJ2014-118">[114]</a></sup> Furthermore, a potentially habitable planet must orbit a stable <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">star</a> at a distance within which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary-mass_object" title="Planetary-mass object">planetary-mass objects</a> with sufficient <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">atmospheric pressure</a> can support <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_water" title="Liquid water">liquid water</a> at their surfaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kopparapu-2013_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-kopparapu-2013-119">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20130503_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20130503-120">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_impact">Cultural impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exoplanet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Cultural impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>On 9 May 2013, a <a class="external text" href="http://science.edgeboss.net/wmedia/science/sst2013/SP050913.wvx" rel="nofollow">congressional hearing</a> by two <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a> <a href="/wiki/United_States_congressional_committee" title="United States congressional committee">subcommittees</a> discussed "<a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20141207205111/https://science.house.gov/hearing/subcommittee-space-and-subcommittee-research-joint-hearing-exoplanet-discoveries-have-we" rel="nofollow">Exoplanet Discoveries: Have We Found Other Earths?</a>", prompted by the discovery of exoplanet <a href="/wiki/Kepler-62f" title="Kepler-62f">Kepler-62f</a>, along with <a href="/wiki/Kepler-62e" title="Kepler-62e">Kepler-62e</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kepler-62c" title="Kepler-62c">Kepler-62c</a>. A related special issue of the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i>, published earlier, described the discovery of the exoplanets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20130503_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20130503-120">[116]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Exoplanet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-footnoteA-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteA_12-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteA_12-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">For the purpose of this 1 in 5 statistic, "Sun-like" means <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G-type_star" title="G-type star">G-type star</a>. Data for Sun-like stars was not available so this statistic is an extrapolation from data about <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K-type_star" title="K-type star">K-type stars</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-footnoteB-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteB_13-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteB_13-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">For the purpose of this 1 in 5 statistic, Earth-sized means 1–2 Earth radii</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-footnoteC-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-footnoteC_14-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For the purpose of this 1 in 5 statistic, "habitable zone" means the region with 0.25 to 4 times Earth's stellar flux (corresponding to 0.5–2 AU for the Sun).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-footnoteD-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-footnoteD_15-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">About 1/4 of stars are GK Sun-like stars. The number of stars in the galaxy is not accurately known, but assuming 200 billion stars in total, the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> would have about 50 billion Sun-like (GK) stars, of which about 1 in 5 (22%) or 11 billion would be Earth-sized in the habitable zone. Including red dwarfs would increase this to 40 billion.</span></li>
